Pure, without impurities or blemishes.

"With lapis lazuli as its ground,
Golden cords bordering its paths,
Seven-jeweled trees of mixed colors
Constantly bearing flowers and fruits.

"The Bodhisattvas of that land,
Their aspirations constantly firm and solid,
Having all perfected
Spiritual powers and pāramitās—

"In the presence of innumerable Buddhas,
They have well studied the Bodhisattva-Way.
Great beings such as these
Are transformed by the Buddha Padmaprabha.

"When the Buddha was a prince,
He renounced his kingdom and worldly glory.
In his very final body
He left home and perfected the Buddha-Way.

"The Buddha Padmaprabha shall dwell in the world
For twelve lesser kalpas.
The people of that land
Shall have lifespans of eight lesser kalpas.

"After the Buddha's extinction,
The correct Dharma shall dwell in the world
For thirty-two lesser kalpas,
Extensively ferrying across all living beings.

"When the correct Dharma is exhausted,
The semblance [Dharma shall last] thirty-two [kalpas].
Śarīra shall be widely distributed,
Universally receiving offerings from devas and humans.

"What the Buddha Padmaprabha accomplishes—
These matters shall all be thus.
That Honored Sage of Two Feet
Shall be most supreme, without equal or match.

"That one shall be none other than you—
You should rejoice and celebrate!"

At that time the fourfold assembly—bhikṣus, bhikṣuṇīs, upāsakas, upāsikās—together with devas, nāgas, yakṣas, gandharvas, asuras, garuḍas, kiṃnaras, mahoragas, and other members of the great assembly, seeing Śāriputra before the Buddha receive the prediction of anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi, experienced great joy in their hearts, leaping with immeasurable delight. Each removed the upper garment they were wearing and offered it to the Buddha. Śakra Devānām Indra, the Brahmā Heavenly King, and others, together with innumerable celestial sons, also made offerings to the Buddha with wondrous celestial garments, celestial mandārava flowers, mahāmandārava flowers, and such. The celestial garments they scattered remained in empty space, revolving of themselves. Celestial music of hundreds of thousands of myriads of kinds simultaneously played in empty space, raining down multitudes of celestial flowers. And they spoke these words: "Long ago at Vārāṇasī the Buddha first turned the Dharma-wheel; now he again turns the supreme and greatest Dharma-wheel!"

At that time the celestial sons, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse, saying:

"Long ago at Vārāṇasī
He turned the Dharma-wheel of the Four Noble Truths,
Making distinctions and expounding all dharmas—
The arising and cessation of the five aggregates.

"Now again he turns the most wondrous,
Supreme and great Dharma-wheel.
This Dharma is extremely deep and mysterious;
Few are able to believe it.

"We from ancient times until now
Have repeatedly heard the World-Honored One expound,
But have never before heard
Such profound, wondrous, and supreme Dharma.

"Since the World-Honored One expounds this Dharma,
We all rejoice accordingly.
The greatly wise Śāriputra
Has now received the honored prediction.

"We too are likewise thus—
We shall certainly attain Buddhahood.
Throughout all the world
Most honored, without any superior.

"The Buddha-Way is inconceivable;
He expounds through skillful means in accordance with what is appropriate.
Whatever merit and karmic activity we possess,
Whether of this life or past lives,

"Together with seeing the Buddha's merit—
All of it we dedicate toward the Buddha-Way."

At that time Śāriputra addressed the Buddha, saying: "World-Honored One, I now have no further doubts or regrets. Personally before the Buddha I have received the prediction of anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi. These twelve hundred whose minds are self-mastered formerly dwelt at the stage of learning. The Buddha constantly taught and transformed them, saying: 'My Dharma can separate one from birth, old age, sickness, and death, ultimately reaching nirvāṇa.' Whether at the stage of learning or beyond learning, these people also each considered that, having separated from the view of self as well as views of existence and non-existence, they had attained nirvāṇa. But now, before the World-Honored One, hearing what they had never before heard, they have all fallen into doubt and perplexity. Excellent, World-Honored One! We pray that for the sake of the fourfold assembly you will explain the cause and condition, causing them to separate from doubt and regret."

At that time the Buddha told Śāriputra: "Did I not previously say that all Buddhas, the World-Honored Ones, through various causes and conditions, parables, and expressions, expound the Dharma through skillful means, all for the sake of anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi? All these expositions are for the sake of transforming Bodhisattvas. However, Śāriputra, I shall now once more through a parable clarify this meaning further. Those with wisdom comprehend through parables.

"Śāriputra, suppose in a certain land, province, or village there was a great elder who was aged and declining, possessing immeasurable wealth, with many fields, estates, and servants. His house was vast and great, having only a single gate. A great many people—one, two, or even five hundred—were dwelling within it. The halls and pavilions were decayed and old, the walls crumbling down, the pillar bases rotten and decomposed, the beams and ridgepoles tilting precariously. All around, simultaneously, fire suddenly broke out, engulfing the dwelling. The elder's sons—perhaps ten, twenty, or as many as thirty—were inside this house.

"The elder, seeing this great fire arise from the four directions, immediately felt great alarm and terror, and had this thought: 'Though I am able to emerge safely through this burning gate, my sons within the burning house are engrossed in play and amusements, unaware, unknowing, unalarmed, unafraid. Though the fire approaches and presses upon their bodies, causing them pain and suffering, their minds harbor no aversion or distress, and they have no thought of escaping.'

"Śāriputra, this elder made this reflection: 'I have physical strength in my body and hands. I could use a robe or a table to carry them out of the dwelling.' Then he further reflected: 'This house has only one gate, which moreover is narrow and small. My sons are young and immature, lacking understanding. They are attached to their place of play and may fall down and be burned by the fire. I should tell them of this frightening matter: this house is already burning—they must quickly get out now, lest they be harmed by the fire.' Having thought thus, in accordance with his reflections, he informed all his sons: 'You must quickly come out!' Though the father showed compassion and skillfully coaxed and admonished them, the sons were engrossed in amusements and play, unwilling to believe and receive [his warning], neither alarmed nor afraid, and completely lacking any thought of escape. Moreover, they did not even know what fire was, what a house was, or what 'losing' meant. They merely ran about from east to west, playing, glancing at their father and nothing more.

"At that time the elder made this reflection: 'This house is already being consumed by a great fire. If my sons and I do not get out in time, we shall certainly be burned. I shall now employ skillful means to cause my sons to escape this calamity.' The father, knowing what each of his sons had previously enjoyed—various rare and unusual playthings and objects to which they would certainly be attached—spoke to them, saying: 'The playthings you enjoy, rare and difficult to obtain—if you do not take them now, you will surely regret it later. Various sheep-carts, deer-carts, and ox-carts are now outside the gate, where you can play with them. You should quickly come out of this burning house. Whatever you desire, I shall give to you all.'

"At that time, the sons, hearing their father describe these precious playthings—things that suited their wishes—each became emboldened and brave. Pushing and jostling one another, they raced competitively forth, scrambling to escape the burning house.

"At that time, when the elder saw that his sons had safely escaped and were all seated in the open at the crossroads, no longer obstructed, his mind became tranquil and he was filled with joy and rapture. Each of the sons then addressed their father, saying: 'Father, the playthings you promised us earlier—the sheep-carts, deer-carts, and ox-carts—please give them to us now.'

"Śāriputra, at that time the elder gave to each of his sons an identical great cart. These carts were tall and spacious, adorned and decorated with manifold jewels, surrounded with railings, with bells hanging from all four sides. A canopy was spread over the top, also decorated and adorned with rare and various jewels. Jeweled cords crisscrossed, with hanging garlands of flowers. Inside, layered mats were spread with cushions of red pillows. They were yoked to white oxen, their skin lustrous and pure, their physical form beautiful, possessing great muscular strength, their gait steady and even, as swift as the wind. Moreover, many servants attended and guarded them. Wherefore was this so? This great elder possessed immeasurable wealth; all his various storehouses were completely full to overflowing. And he made this reflection: 'My wealth is limitless. I should not give inferior small carts to my sons. These young children are all my sons, whom I love without partiality. Possessing such great seven-jeweled carts, immeasurable in number, I should with equal mind give one to each, without showing favoritism. Wherefore is this so? Even if I were to distribute these possessions throughout an entire land, there would still be no shortage—how much more so for my sons!' At that time, each of the sons mounted his great cart, experiencing what they had never before experienced, beyond their original expectations.

"Śāriputra, what is your opinion? Did that elder, in giving precious great carts equally to his sons, commit any falsehood or not?"

Śāriputra replied: "No, World-Honored One. That elder merely enabled his sons to escape the fire's calamity, preserving their bodies and lives—this was not falsehood. Why is this so? Precisely by preserving their bodies and lives, they had already obtained wonderful playthings—how much more so through skillful means used to rescue them from that burning house! World-Honored One, even if that elder had not given them so much as the smallest cart, it still would not have been falsehood. Why is this so? That elder had previously formed this intention: 'I shall employ skillful means to cause my sons to escape.' For this reason, there is no falsehood. How much less falsehood is there when the elder, knowing himself to possess immeasurable wealth, wishing to benefit his sons equally, gave them each great carts!"

The Buddha told Śāriputra: "Excellent, excellent! It is just as you have said. Śāriputra, the Tathāgata is also likewise thus—he is the father of all the world. Regarding all fear, decline, distress, anxiety, ignorance, and dark obscuration, he has forever exhausted them without remainder, and has perfected immeasurable insight and vision, powers, fearlessnesses, great spiritual power, and the power of wisdom. He possesses perfect skillful means and the pāramitā of wisdom. His great compassion and great kindness are constant and unwearying; he eternally seeks wholesome matters, benefiting all.

"Thus he is born into the triple world—a decaying and burning house—in order to ferry across living beings from birth, old age, sickness, death, grief, sorrow, suffering, distress, delusion, darkness, and the three poisons of fire. He teaches and transforms them, causing them to attain anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi. He sees all living beings being seared and boiled by birth, old age, sickness, death, grief, sorrow, suffering, and distress. Moreover, due to the five desires and wealth they undergo various kinds of suffering. Further, through greedy attachment and pursuit, they presently undergo manifold sufferings and afterward undergo the sufferings of the hells, animals, and hungry ghosts. Even if born in the heavens or in the human realm, they undergo the sufferings of poverty and destitution, the suffering of separation from the beloved, the suffering of encountering the hateful—such various kinds of suffering as these. Yet living beings, sunken within all this, delight and play joyfully, unaware, unknowing, unalarmed, unafraid. They also do not become weary or seek liberation. In this burning house of the triple world they run about from east to west. Though they encounter great suffering, they do not regard it as affliction.

"Śāriputra, having seen this, the Buddha then made this reflection: 'I am the father of living beings. I should extract them from suffering and distress and give them the joy of the immeasurable, limitless Buddha-wisdom, causing them to find amusement in it.'

"Śāriputra, the Tathāgata further made this reflection: 'If I merely use spiritual power and the power of wisdom, casting aside skillful means, and for the sake of living beings praise the Tathāgata's insight and vision, powers, and fearlessnesses, living beings will be unable to be ferried across through this. Wherefore is this so? These living beings have not yet escaped birth, old age, sickness, death, grief, sorrow, suffering, and distress. They are being burned by the burning house of the triple world—how could they comprehend the Buddha's wisdom?'

"Śāriputra, just as that elder, though he had physical strength and power in his body and hands, did not use it but merely through diligent skillful means rescued his sons from the calamity of the burning house, and afterward gave each the precious great carts—the Tathāgata is also likewise thus. Though he possesses power and fearlessness, he does not use it. He merely through the power of wisdom and skillful means extracts and rescues living beings from the burning house of the triple world, expounding for them the Three Vehicles—those of śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, and Buddhas. And he speaks thus: 'You should not delight in dwelling in the burning house of the triple world. Do not greedily crave crude and inferior forms, sounds, smells, tastes, and tangibles. If through greedy attachment you generate love, you will be burned by it. You should quickly exit the triple world and obtain the Three Vehicles of śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, and Buddhas. I now guarantee this matter for you; it shall never prove false. You should merely be diligently vigorous.'

"Through these skillful means the Tathāgata entices living beings forward. He furthermore says: 'You should know that these Three Vehicle Dharmas are all praised by sages. They are free, unbound, without anything upon which they depend or which they seek. Riding these Three Vehicles, through outflow-free faculties, powers, limbs of enlightenment, factors of the Path, dhyāna concentrations, liberations, samādhis, and such, you yourselves experience amusement and thereby attain immeasurable peace, security, and joy.'

"Śāriputra, if there are living beings who internally possess the nature of wisdom, who, hearing the Dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, believe and receive it, who are diligent and vigorous, wishing to quickly exit the triple world and seeking nirvāṇa for themselves—these are called those of the Śrāvaka Vehicle, like those sons who sought the sheep-carts and exited the burning house.

"If there are living beings who, hearing the Dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, believe and receive it, who are diligent and vigorous, seeking self-arising wisdom, delighting in solitary goodness and tranquility, and deeply understanding the causes and conditions of all dharmas—these are called those of the Pratyekabuddha Vehicle, like those sons who sought the deer-carts and exited the burning house.

"If there are living beings who, hearing the Dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, believe and receive it, who diligently cultivate and vigorously advance, seeking the wisdom of all modes, Buddha-wisdom, self-arising wisdom, teacherless wisdom, the Tathāgata's insight and vision, powers, and fearlessnesses, who pity, bring peace, and benefit immeasurable multitudes of living beings, benefiting devas and humans and ferrying across all—these are called those of the Great Vehicle. Because Bodhisattvas seek this Vehicle, they are called Mahāsattvas, like those sons who sought the ox-carts and exited the burning house.

"Śāriputra, just as that elder, seeing his sons safely escape the burning house and reach a place without fear, reflecting on his immeasurable wealth, gave great carts equally to his sons—the Tathāgata is also likewise thus, being the father of all living beings. If he sees immeasurable billions of thousands of myriads of living beings, through the Buddha's teaching-gateway, escape the suffering, fear, and dangerous paths of the triple world and attain the joy of nirvāṇa, the Tathāgata at that time makes this reflection: 'I possess immeasurable, limitless wisdom, powers, fearlessnesses, and other treasuries of Buddha-Dharmas. All these living beings are my sons. I give equally to all the Great Vehicle, not allowing anyone to individually attain extinction. All shall attain extinction through the Tathāgata's extinction.' Those living beings who escape the triple world are all given the amusements and tools of the Buddhas' dhyāna concentrations and liberations. They are all of one mark, one kind, praised by sages, able to generate pure, wondrous, foremost joy.

"Śāriputra, just as that elder first used the three carts to entice his sons, but afterward gave them only the great cart adorned with jewels, safest and foremost—yet that elder is not guilty of falsehood—the Tathāgata is also likewise thus, without any falsehood. First he expounds the Three Vehicles to guide living beings, but afterward he ferries them across solely through the Great Vehicle. Wherefore is this so? The Tathāgata possesses the treasury of immeasurable wisdom, powers, fearlessnesses, and all dharmas, and is able to give all living beings the Dharma of the Great Vehicle. However, not all are able to receive it.

"Śāriputra, for this cause and condition you should know: through the power of skillful means, within the One Buddha Vehicle, the Buddhas make distinctions and expound three."

The Buddha, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse, saying:

"Suppose there is an elder
Who has a great dwelling,
Which, being of long standing,
Moreover is now dilapidated and ruinous.

"The halls and houses are lofty and dangerous,
The pillar bases decayed and rotten,
The beams and ridgepoles tilting askew,
The foundations and steps crumbling and destroyed.

"Walls and partitions are cracked and gaping,
The mud coating has peeled and fallen off,
The thatch covering is scattered and collapsing,
The rafters and crossbeams are loose and dislodged.

"Surroundings winding and crooked,
Filth fills them everywhere.
Five hundred people
Dwell and reside within it.

"Kites, owls, eagles, and vultures,
Crows, magpies, doves, and pigeons,
Vipers, snakes, vipers, and scorpions,
Centipedes and millipedes,

"Geckos and hundred-leggers,
Weasels, raccoons, and mice—
Various evil insect tribes
Run crisscross back and forth.

"Places of excrement and urine stench,
With impure matter flowing and overflowing—
Dung beetles and other insects
Gather and collect upon them.

"Foxes, wolves, and wild dogs
Chew, trample, and gnaw at corpses,
Scattering bones and flesh about.
Due to this, packs of dogs

"Competitively come to snatch and grab,
Starving, emaciated, frightened, and frantic,
Everywhere seeking food,
Fighting and struggling, tearing and pulling,

"Snarling and howling, barking—
Such is the fearful condition
And changing state of that dwelling.

"Everywhere throughout
Are sprites and demons.
Yakṣas and evil ghosts
Devour human flesh.

"Poisonous insect tribes
And various evil birds and beasts
Hatch and bear offspring,
Each hiding and protecting them.

"Yakṣas compete to come
And struggle to seize and devour them.
Having eaten their fill,
Their evil minds become even fiercer.

"The sounds of their fighting and struggling
Are extremely fearful.
Kumbhāṇḍa ghosts
Squat upon mounds of earth,

"Sometimes rising from the ground
One or two feet,
Wandering back and forth,
Indulging in frivolous play,

"Seizing dogs by both feet,
Striking them so they lose their voice,
Placing feet upon their necks,
Terrifying the dogs for their own amusement.

"Again there are various ghosts
Whose bodies are tall and large,
Naked, black, and emaciated,
Constantly dwelling therein,

"Emitting great evil sounds,
Crying and calling out for food.
Again there are ghosts
Whose throats are like needles.

"Again there are ghosts
With heads like ox heads—
Some eat human flesh,
Others also devour dogs.

"Hair disheveled and wild,
Cruel and dangerous in harming,
Oppressed by hunger and thirst,
They cry out and run about.

"Yakṣas, hungry ghosts,
And various evil birds and beasts,
Desperately hungry, facing in all directions,
Peering through windows and openings—

"Such difficulties as these,
And fears immeasurable,
Belong to this decayed old dwelling,
Which is owned by one person.

"That person had recently gone out
Not long before
When, in the rear of that dwelling,
Fire suddenly broke out.

"Simultaneously on all four sides
Its flames burned fiercely together.
Ridgepoles, beams, rafters, and pillars
Exploded with震裂 [震crackling sounds],

"Crumbling, breaking, and falling down,
Walls and partitions collapsing.
All the ghosts and spirits
Raised their voices in great cries.

"Eagles, vultures, and various birds,
Kumbhāṇḍas and such,
In frantic terror and fear,
Were unable to escape by themselves.

"Evil beasts and poisonous insects
Hid and burrowed into holes and crevices.
Piśāca ghosts
Also dwelt therein.

"Due to scant merit and virtue,
Oppressed by the fire,
They mutually harmed one another,
Drinking blood and devouring flesh.

"Wild dog tribes
Had already died before.
Various large evil beasts
Competed to come and devour them.

"Foul smoke and flames
Filled and blocked all four sides.
Centipedes and millipedes,
Poisonous snake tribes,

"Burned by the fire,
Struggled to exit their holes.
Kumbhāṇḍa ghosts
Seized and devoured them.

"Moreover, all the hungry ghosts
Had fire burning atop their heads.
Afflicted by hunger, thirst, heat, and distress,
They frantically ran about in anguish.

"Such is the condition of that dwelling—
Extremely fearful,
With calamities of poison, harm, and fire,
Manifold difficulties, not just one.

"At that time the master of the dwelling
Was standing outside the gate.
He heard someone say:
'Your various sons

"'Earlier, due to play and amusement,
Entered into this dwelling.
Young and lacking knowledge,
They delight and are attached to amusements.'

"The elder, hearing this,
Alarmed, entered the burning house,
Appropriately planning rescue,
To prevent them from being burned or harmed.

"He admonished and instructed his sons,
Explaining the manifold troubles and difficulties:
'Evil ghosts and poisonous insects,
The calamity of fire spreading everywhere,

"'Manifold sufferings continuously,
One after another without cease.
Poisonous snakes and vipers,
Together with all the yakṣas,

"'Kumbhāṇḍa ghosts,
Wild dogs, foxes, and jackals,
Eagles, vultures, and kites,
And hundred-legged tribes,

"'Afflicted by hunger and thirst,
Extremely fearful—
This is a place of suffering and difficulty,
How much worse with the great fire!'

"The sons, lacking knowledge,
Though hearing their father's instruction,
Still remained attached and delighted,
Playing without cease.

"At that time the elder
Made this reflection:
'The sons are like this,
Increasing my grief and distress.

"'Now in this dwelling
There is nothing delightful,
Yet these sons,
Engrossed in frivolous play,

"'Do not receive my teaching
And will be harmed by the fire.'
He immediately reflected,
Establishing various skillful means,

"And told all his sons:
'I have various
Precious playthings—
Wonderful jeweled fine carts,

"'Sheep-carts and deer-carts,
And great ox-carts,
Now outside the gate.
Come out, you!

"'For your sake
I have created these carts.
According to your desires,
You can play with them.'

"Hearing these carts described,
The sons immediately raced competitively,
Running forth and emerging,
Reaching the open ground,

"Separating from all suffering and difficulties.
The elder, seeing his sons
Escape the burning house,
Dwelling at the crossroads,

"Seated on lion thrones,
Made this joyful declaration:
'I am now happy and at ease!
These sons

"'Were extremely difficult to give birth to and raise.
Foolish, small, and lacking knowledge,
They entered a dangerous dwelling
With many poisonous insects

"'And frightful sprites and demons.
The great fire with fierce flames
Arose simultaneously from all four sides,
Yet these sons,

"'Greedy and attached to play,
I have now rescued them,
Causing them to escape difficulty.
Therefore, all people,

"'I am now happy and at ease.'
At that time the sons,
Knowing their father was seated securely,
All approached their father's place

"And addressed their father, saying:
'Please grant us
The three kinds of jeweled carts
As you previously promised:

"'When the sons emerge,
You shall give three carts
According to our desires.'
Now is precisely the time—

"'We only pray you will bestow them!'
The elder, greatly wealthy,
With many treasuries and storehouses—
Gold, silver, lapis lazuli,

"Giant clam shell, and agate—
With manifold jeweled materials
He fashioned various great carts,
Decorated and adorned,

"Surrounded with railings,
With bells hanging from four sides,
And golden cords crisscrossing.
Pearl netting

"Spread and extended above,
With golden flower garlands
Hanging down everywhere.
Multicolored decorations

"Surrounded them all about.
Soft silk and cotton
Served as cushions and mats.
Superior, wondrous fine cloth

"Worth thousands of billions,
Pure white and immaculately clean,
Covered them above.
There were great white oxen,

"Fat, strong, and powerful,
Beautiful in physical form,
Yoked to the jeweled carts,
With many attendants

"Serving and guarding them.
With these wonderful carts
He gave equally to his sons.
The sons at that time

"Experienced joy and rapture,
Mounting these jeweled carts,
Roaming in the four directions,
Playing joyfully,

"At ease without obstruction.
I tell Śāriputra:
I too am likewise thus—
Among sages most honored,

"Father of the world.
All living beings
Are all my sons,
Deeply attached to worldly pleasures,

"Without minds of wisdom.
The triple world has no peace—
It is like a burning house,
Filled with manifold sufferings,

"Extremely fearful,
Constantly having birth, old age,
Sickness, death, and anxieties—
Fires such as these

"Blaze fiercely without cease.
The Tathāgata has already separated
From the burning house of the triple world,
Dwelling serenely and leisurely at ease,

"Peacefully residing in forests and wilds.
Now this triple world
Is all my possession.
The living beings within it

"Are all my sons.
Yet now this place
Has many troubles and difficulties.
Only I alone

"Am able to provide rescue and protection.
Though I furthermore teach and instruct,
They do not believe or receive it,
Due to being deeply attached

"To desires through greed.
Through these skillful means
I expound the Three Vehicles,
Causing all living beings

"To know the triple world's sufferings,
Demonstrating and expounding
The transcendent worldly path.
If these sons

"Have minds that are resolute and determined,
Perfecting the three clarities
And the six spiritual powers,
Some attaining pratyekabuddhahood,

"And irreversible Bodhisattvas—
Śāriputra,
For the sake of living beings,
Through this parable

"I expound the One Buddha Vehicle.
If you are able
To believe and receive these words,
You shall all

"Attain accomplishment of the Buddha-Way.
This Vehicle is wondrous,
Pure and foremost.
Throughout all worlds

"There is none superior to it—
That which the Buddha delights in.
All living beings
Should praise it,

"Make offerings to it, and bow to it.
Immeasurable billions of thousands
Of powers and liberations,
Dhyāna concentrations and wisdom,

"Together with the Buddha's other dharmas—
Having attained such a Vehicle,
It causes all the sons
Throughout days, nights, and kalpa-periods

"Constantly to find amusement and play,
Together with Bodhisattvas
And the multitude of śrāvakas,
Mounting this jeweled Vehicle,

"Going directly to the place of enlightenment.
For this cause and condition,
Seeking throughout the ten directions,
There is no other Vehicle,

"Except the Buddha's skillful means.
I tell Śāriputra
That you and all others
Are all my sons,

"And I am your father.
Throughout accumulated kalpas
You have been burned by manifold sufferings,
And I have rescued and extracted you all,

"Causing you to exit the triple world.
Though I previously said
You had all attained extinction,
You have merely exhausted birth and death

"But have not truly become extinct.
What you should now accomplish
Is solely Buddha-wisdom.
If there are Bodhisattvas

"Within this assembly
Who can single-mindedly listen to
The true Dharma of all Buddhas—
Though the Buddhas, the World-Honored Ones,

"Through skillful means
Transform living beings,
All those transformed are Bodhisattvas.

"If people have small wisdom,
Deeply attached to craving and desire—
For ones such as these
I expound the Truth of Suffering.

"Living beings' minds rejoice,
Experiencing what they never before had:
'The Buddha's exposition of the Truth of Suffering
Is true and without difference!'

"If there are living beings
Who do not know suffering's root,
Who are deeply attached to suffering's cause
And cannot temporarily relinquish it—

"For ones such as these,
I expound the Path through skillful means.
The cause of all sufferings
Is greed and desire as the root.

"If one extinguishes greed and desire,
It has nothing upon which to depend.
Extinguishing and exhausting all sufferings
Is called the Third Truth.

"For the sake of the Truth of Extinction,
One cultivates and practices the Path.
Separating from all bonds of suffering
Is called attaining liberation.

"Regarding what matter
Do these people attain liberation?
They merely separate from falsity and emptiness—
This is called liberation.

"In reality they have not yet attained
Complete liberation.
The Buddha says these people
Have not truly attained extinction,

"Because these people have not yet attained
The supreme Way.
My intention does not wish
To cause them to reach extinction.

"I am the Dharma King,
Sovereign over all dharmas.
To bring peace to living beings,
I therefore appear in the world.

"Śāriputra,
This Dharma-seal of mine,
Wishing to benefit
The world, I expound it.

"Wherever you roam,
Do not transmit it carelessly.
If there are those who hear it
And rejoice, receiving it reverently,

"You should know such people
Are irreversible [from enlightenment].
If there are those who believe and receive
This sutra-Dharma,

"Such people have already
Seen Buddhas of the past,
Reverently making offerings
And also hearing this Dharma.

"If people are able
To believe what you expound,
They thereby see me
And also see you

"Together with the bhikṣu Saṅgha
And all the Bodhisattvas.
This Dharma Flower Sutra
Is expounded for those of deep wisdom.

"Those of shallow knowledge hearing it
Become confused and fail to comprehend.
All śrāvakas
And pratyekabuddhas

"Regarding this sutra
Find it beyond their power's reach.
Even you, Śāriputra,
Regarding this sutra

"Gained entry through faith—
How much more so other śrāvakas!
The other śrāvakas,
Due to believing the Buddha's words,

"Comply with this sutra,
Not through their own wisdom's portion.
Furthermore, Śāriputra,
To the arrogant and lazy,

"Those who calculate [views of] self—
Do not expound this sutra.
Common people of shallow knowledge,
Deeply attached to the five desires,

"Hearing it, cannot comprehend—
Also do not expound it to them.
If people do not believe
And slander this sutra,

"They thereby sever all
The Buddha-seeds of the world.
Or they may frown with furrowed brows
And harbor doubts and perplexities—

"You should now listen
To the karmic retribution of such people:
Whether the Buddha is in the world
Or after his extinction,

"If there are those who slander
Sutras such as this,
Who see those reading, reciting,
Or upholding and copying the sutra,

"Who despise, hate, envy them,
And harbor resentful thoughts—
Listen now to the karmic retribution
Of such people:

"When their lives end,
They enter Avīci hell,
Fully remaining for one kalpa.
When the kalpa ends they are reborn,

"Turning and revolving thus
Through innumerable kalpas.
Emerging from the hells,
They shall fall into the animal realm—

"As dogs or wild jackals,
Their forms gaunt and emaciated,
Dark, mottled with mange and scabies,
Objects for people to torment.

"Moreover, they shall be
Loathed and despised by people,
Constantly afflicted by hunger and thirst,
Their bones and flesh withered and desiccated.

"In life receiving cruel torment,
In death struck by tiles and stones—
Due to severing the Buddha-seed,
They receive such karmic retribution.

"If they become camels
Or are born among donkeys,
Their bodies constantly bearing heavy loads,
With sticks and whips added,

"They think only of water and grass,
Knowing nothing else.
Due to slandering this sutra,
They obtain retribution such as this.

"Some become wild jackals
Entering villages and settlements,
Their bodies afflicted with mange and scabies,
Moreover lacking one eye,

"Struck and pelted
By various children,
Receiving all manner of suffering and pain,
Sometimes even unto death.

"Having died in this [state],
They again receive python bodies,
Their forms long and large,
Five hundred yojanas,

"Deaf, stupid, and without feet,
Writhing and crawling on their bellies.
By various small insects
They are bitten and devoured,

"Day and night undergoing suffering
Without any respite.
Due to slandering this sutra,
They obtain retribution such as this.

"If they attain human form,
All their faculties are dull and dark—
Dwarfed, hunched, lame, and paralyzed,
Blind, deaf, and hunchbacked.

"Whatever they say,
People do not believe or receive.
Their breath is constantly foul;
They are possessed by ghosts and demons.

"Poor and lowly,
Employed by others,
Afflicted with many illnesses, emaciated,
Without anyone to rely upon.

"Though they attach themselves to people,
People pay them no mind.
If they obtain anything,
They soon again forget and lose it.

"If they practice the medical way,
Following prescriptions to treat illness,
They further increase others' diseases
Or also cause death.

"If they themselves have illness,
No one rescues or treats them.
Though they take good medicine,
It further increases their suffering.

"If others rebel,
Plundering and robbing through theft—
Transgressions such as these
Befall them as calamities.

"Such sinful people
Forever never see the Buddha,
The King of Multitudes of Sages,
Expounding Dharma and teaching transformation.

"Such sinful people
Are constantly born in difficult places—
Mad, deaf, with minds confused,
Forever never hearing the Dharma.

"Throughout innumerable kalpas
Numerous as Ganges' sands,
Whenever born, deaf and mute,
Their faculties incomplete,

"They constantly dwell in the hells
As if roaming in parks and gardens.
In other evil paths
As if in their own dwelling places.

"Camels, donkeys, pigs, and dogs—
These are their places of movement.
Due to slandering this sutra,
They obtain retribution such as this.

"If they attain human form,
Deaf, blind, mute, and dumb,
Poverty and all manner of decline
Serve as their adornments.

"Dropsy, emaciation,
Scabies, ulcers, and boils—
Such diseases as these
Serve as their clothing.

"Their bodies constantly stink and are foul,
Filthy and impure.
Deeply attached to the view of self,
They increase in anger and rage.

"Sexual desire burns fiercely—
They do not distinguish birds from beasts.
Due to slandering this sutra,
They obtain retribution such as this.

"I tell Śāriputra:
Those who slander this sutra—
If I were to speak of their transgressions,
I could not exhaust them throughout a kalpa.

"For this cause and condition,
I therefore tell you:
Among people without wisdom,
Do not expound this sutra.

"If there are those of keen faculties,
Whose wisdom is clear and luminous,
Much learning and strong retention,
Seeking the Buddha-Way—

"For people such as these,
One may expound it.
If people have seen
Billions of hundreds of thousands of Buddhas,

"Having planted all manner of wholesome roots,
With profound minds firm and solid—
For people such as these,
One may expound it.

"If people are vigorously advancing,
Constantly cultivating compassionate minds,
Not begrudging body or life—
For such one may expound it.

"If people are reverent,
Without any divergent minds,
Separated from all common fools,
Dwelling alone in mountains and marshlands—

"For people such as these,
One may expound it.
Furthermore, Śāriputra,
If you see someone

"Who has renounced evil acquaintances
And drawn near to good friends—
For people such as these,
One may expound it.

"If you see Buddha-sons
Upholding precepts purely,
Like pure, bright jewels,
Seeking Great Vehicle sutras—

"For people such as these,
One may expound it.
If people are without anger,
Straightforward, gentle, and pliant,

"Constantly pitying all,
Revering all Buddhas—
For people such as these,
One may expound it.

"Again, there are Buddha-sons
Who, within great assemblies,
With pure minds,
Through various causes and conditions,

"Parables and expressions,
Expound Dharma without obstruction—
For people such as these,
One may expound it.

"If there are bhikṣus
Who, for the sake of the wisdom of all modes,
Seek the Dharma in the four directions,
Joining palms and reverently receiving it,

"Who merely delight in receiving and upholding
Great Vehicle sutras,
Not even receiving
A single verse of other sutras—

"For people such as these,
One may expound it.
Like one who single-mindedly
Seeks the Buddha's śarīra,

"Seeking sutras in such manner,
Having obtained them, reverently receiving them on the crown of the head—
Such a person no longer
Aspires to seek other sutras,

"Nor has ever thought of
Non-Buddhist scriptures—
For people such as these,
One may expound it.

"I tell Śāriputra:
If I were to speak of the characteristics
Of those seeking the Buddha-Way,
I could not exhaust them throughout a kalpa.

"People such as these
Are then able to believe and comprehend.
You should expound for them
The Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma."


═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

CHAPTER FOUR: FAITH AND UNDERSTANDING

At that time the Venerable Subhūti, Mahākātyāyana, Mahākāśyapa, and Mahāmaudgalyāyana, hearing from the Buddha this Dharma never before heard, and hearing that the World-Honored One conferred upon Śāriputra the prediction of anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi, gave rise to a rare aspiration. Joyful and rapturous, they immediately rose from their seats, adjusted their robes, bared their right shoulders, placed their right knees upon the ground, single-mindedly joined their palms, bent their bodies in reverence, gazed up at the Honored Countenance, and addressed the Buddha, saying:

"We dwell at the head of the Saṅgha, and our years are all declining and aged. Considering ourselves to have already attained nirvāṇa, we felt ourselves incompetent and did not further advance to seek anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi. World-Honored One, you have long expounded the Dharma. At that time we sat in the assembly, our bodies growing weary and lax, merely contemplating emptiness, signlessness, and wishlessness. Regarding the Dharma of the Bodhisattvas—the spiritual penetrations through which they play, the purification of Buddha-lands, and the perfection of living beings—our minds took no delight or joy in these. Wherefore is this so? The World-Honored One caused us to emerge from the triple world and attain the realization of nirvāṇa. Moreover, we are now aged and declining. Regarding the Buddha's teaching and transformation of Bodhisattvas toward anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi, we did not generate even a single thought of delight or aspiration.

"We now before the Buddha, hearing the conferral of anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi upon śrāvakas, experience extreme joy in our hearts and have attained what we never before attained. We did not expect that now, suddenly, we would hear this rare Dharma. We deeply congratulate ourselves on having obtained this great wholesome benefit—immeasurable jewels arriving unsought."

"World-Honored One, we now delight in speaking a parable to clarify this meaning. Suppose there was a person who, while still young in years, abandoned his father, ran away, and for a long time dwelt in another land—perhaps ten, twenty, or as many as fifty years. As his years increased and he grew older, he moreover became impoverished and destitute, running about in the four directions seeking clothing and food. Gradually wandering and traveling, he happened to move toward his original land.

"His father had previously searched for his son but failed to find him, and had settled in a certain city. His household was greatly wealthy, with treasuries and wealth immeasurable—gold, silver, lapis lazuli, coral, amber, crystal, pearls, and such. His various storehouses were all overflowing and full. He had many servants, ministers, assistants, and subjects, as well as elephants, horses, carriages, oxen, and sheep—immeasurable in number. His investments and profits extended throughout other lands, and merchants and traders were also extremely numerous.

"At that time the impoverished son wandered through various villages, passing through states and cities, until he finally arrived at the city where his father had settled. The father's thoughts of his son—they had been separated for over fifty years—yet he had never spoken of this matter to anyone. He merely contemplated it himself, his mind harboring regret and resentment. He reflected to himself: 'I am old and declining. I possess much wealth—gold, silver, and precious jewels. My storehouses are overflowing and full. Yet I have no son or heir. Should I suddenly die one day, my wealth and possessions will scatter and be lost, with no one to entrust them to.' For this reason he earnestly and constantly thought of his son. He furthermore made this reflection: 'If I could obtain my son and entrust my wealth to him, I would be content, happy, and joyful, with no further cares or anxieties.'

"World-Honored One, at that time the impoverished son, hiring himself out and wandering from place to place, happened to arrive at his father's house. He stopped and stood by the gate. From afar he saw his father seated upon a lion throne, his footstool adorned with jewels, with brāhmaṇas, kṣatriyas, and householders all reverently surrounding him. His body was adorned with pearl necklaces worth thousands of myriads. Officials, servants, and attendants, holding white whisks, stood in attendance to his left and right. A jeweled canopy was spread above him, with hanging flowered banners. Fragrant water sprinkled the ground, and scattered renowned flowers. Precious objects were arranged in rows, being brought in and taken out. Such various adornments as these displayed his威德 [awesome virtue] as especially honored.

"The impoverished son, seeing his father possessed of such great power and influence, immediately became fearful and afraid, regretting having come to this place. Secretly he made this thought: 'This person may be a king or equal to a king. This is not a place where I, hiring out my labor, can obtain anything. It would be better to go to an impoverished neighborhood where, exerting my strength, there will be a place [for me], and clothing and food will be easily obtained. If I remain here long, I may be oppressed and forced to work.' Having made this thought, he quickly ran away.

"At that time the wealthy elder, seated upon his lion throne, saw his son and immediately recognized him. His heart experienced great joy. He immediately made this reflection: 'My wealth, treasuries, and storehouses now have someone to whom they can be entrusted! I have constantly thought of this son, with no way to see him, yet suddenly he has come of his own accord—this greatly suits my wishes. Although I am old and declining, I still have greedy attachment [to my possessions].' He immediately dispatched someone nearby to quickly pursue and bring him back.

"At that time the messenger rushed forth and seized him. The impoverished son, alarmed and astonished, cried out in grievance and wailed loudly: 'I have committed no offense against you—why am I being seized?' The messenger held him even more urgently, forcibly dragging him back. At that time the impoverished son thought to himself: 'Though guiltless, I am being imprisoned and seized. I shall certainly die.' He became even more terrified and frightened, fainting and collapsing upon the ground.

"The father, seeing this from afar, spoke to the messenger, saying: 'I have no need of this person. Do not forcibly bring him. Sprinkle cold water on his face to cause him to regain consciousness, but do not speak to him further.' Wherefore was this so? The father knew that his son's aspiration and intention were inferior and lowly. He knew himself to be noble and wealthy, [a status] difficult for his son [to accept]. He was certain this was his son, yet through skillful means he did not tell others, 'This is my son.'

"The messenger spoke to him: 'I now release you. Go wherever you wish.' The impoverished son, joyful and having experienced what he had never before experienced, rose from the ground and went to the impoverished neighborhood to seek clothing and food.

"At that time the elder, wishing to entice his son, established a skillful means. He secretly dispatched two people—haggard and wan in appearance and color, without威德 [dignity or virtue]—[saying]: 'You may go to that place and gently speak to the impoverished son: \"There is work to be done here, and we will pay you double wages.\" If the impoverished son agrees, bring him and put him to work. If he asks what work is to be done, then you may tell him, \"We are hiring you to clear away excrement. We two shall also work together with you.\"'

"At that time the two messengers immediately sought out the impoverished son. Having already found him, they fully related the above matter. At that time the impoverished son first received his wages, then proceeded to clear away excrement.

"His father, seeing his son, pitied yet marveled at him. On another day, through a window, he saw from afar his son's body—emaciated, haggard, and wan, soiled with excrement, dirt, and dust, filthy and impure. He immediately removed his pearl necklaces, fine soft upper garments, and ornamental adornments, and instead put on coarse, shabby, and greasy soiled clothing, his body covered with dust. In his right hand he grasped an implement for clearing excrement, appearing to be fearful. He spoke to the workers: 'You should all work diligently! Do not become lax or idle.' Through this skillful means, he was able to draw near to his son.

"Later he furthermore told him: 'Hey there, fellow! You should constantly work here. Do not go elsewhere again. I shall increase your wages. Whatever you need—bowls, utensils, rice, flour, salt, vinegar, and such—do not hesitate to ask. There are also old and worn-out servants available if needed. Set your mind at ease. I am like your father—you need no longer worry or be anxious. Wherefore is this so? I am old and aged, while you are young and strong. Whenever you work, you are never deceitful, idle, angry, resentful, or complaining. I have never seen you possess such evils as these, unlike other workers. From this day forward, you shall be like my own son.'

"Immediately the elder gave him a new name, calling him his son. At that time the impoverished son, though pleased by this encounter, still considered himself a hired menial worker. For this reason, for twenty years he was constantly made to clear away excrement. After this had passed, their minds mutually trusted one another. He could enter and exit freely without difficulty, yet the place where he stayed was still his original quarters.

"World-Honored One, at that time the elder became ill. Knowing he would soon die, he spoke to the impoverished son, saying: 'I now possess much gold, silver, and precious jewels. My storehouses are overflowing and full. Regarding how much is in them and what should be taken out or brought in, you know all of this completely. My mind is thus—you should understand this intention. Wherefore is this so? Now I and you are not different. You should be even more careful not to allow any loss or waste.'

"At that time the impoverished son immediately received this instruction and command, knowing about all the possessions—gold, silver, precious jewels, and all the storehouses—yet he had not the slightest thought of hoping to acquire even a single meal from them. However, where he stayed was still his original place, and his inferior and lowly mind he also had not yet been able to renounce.

"After a short time had again passed, the father, knowing his son's mind had gradually become open and pervasive, accomplishing great aspirations and feeling disdain for his former mind, approached the time of his end and summoned his son, assembling together relatives, clan members, the king, great ministers, kṣatriyas, and householders, all of whom had already gathered. He then made this proclamation: 'Gentlemen, you should know: this is my son, born of me. In such-and-such a city he abandoned me and ran away, enduring hardship and suffering for over fifty years. His original name was such-and-such, and my name is such-and-such. Long ago in my original city, harboring worry, I searched for him. Suddenly in this place we encountered one another and were reunited. This truly is my son, and I truly am his father. Now all the wealth I possess entirely belongs to my son. What was previously brought out or taken in—all of it was known by my son.'

"World-Honored One, when the impoverished son heard his father speak these words, he experienced great joy, having attained what he had never before attained, and made this reflection: 'Originally I had no mind and harbored no hopes or seeking, yet now this treasury has come of itself.'"

"World-Honored One, the greatly wealthy elder is the Tathāgata. We are all like Buddha-sons. The Tathāgata constantly says we are his sons. World-Honored One, due to the three kinds of suffering, within birth and death we have undergone all manner of heat and torment, being deluded, ignorant, and delighting in attachment to lesser dharmas. Today the World-Honored One causes us to contemplate and eliminate the excrement of the frivolous discussions of all dharmas. Within this we diligently applied vigor and attained nirvāṇa as our day's wages. Having attained this, our minds experienced great joy, considering ourselves satisfied, and we then declared to ourselves: 'Due to our diligent vigor within the Buddha-Dharma, what we have obtained is vast and abundant.'

"Yet the World-Honored One, knowing previously that our minds were attached to shabby desires and delighted in lesser dharmas, then allowed us to go our own way and did not make distinctions for us: 'You should all have a share in the Tathāgata's treasury of insight and vision.' The World-Honored One, through the power of skillful means, has expounded the Tathāgata's wisdom. We, receiving from the Buddha nirvāṇa as our day's wages, considered this a great attainment. Regarding this Great Vehicle, we had no aspiration to seek it.

"We furthermore, because of the Tathāgata's wisdom, demonstrated and expounded it for the Bodhisattvas, yet regarding this ourselves had no aspiration or wish. Wherefore is this so? The Buddha, knowing that our minds delighted in lesser dharmas, through the power of skillful means expounded in accordance with us, and we did not know we were truly Buddha-sons.

"Now we finally know that the World-Honored One regarding Buddha-wisdom is without any stinginess whatsoever. Wherefore is this so? From ancient times until now, we truly have been Buddha-sons, yet we merely delighted in lesser dharmas. Had we possessed minds delighting in the great, the Buddha would have expounded the Great Vehicle Dharma for us.

"Within this sutra, only the One Vehicle is expounded. Yet in the past, before the Bodhisattvas, [the Buddha] disparaged śrāvakas who delight in lesser dharmas. However, in reality the Buddha taught and transformed through the Great Vehicle. Therefore we say: Originally we had no minds harboring hopes or seeking anything. Now the Dharma King's great treasure has arrived of itself. What Buddha-sons should obtain, we have all already obtained."

At that time Mahākāśyapa, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse, saying:

"We today
Hearing the Buddha's sound and teaching,
Experience joy and rapture,
Having attained what we never before had.

"The Buddha says śrāvakas
Shall attain Buddhahood.
The supreme jeweled assembly—
Arrived unsought.

"It is like a young child,
Young and without understanding,
Who abandoned his father and ran away,
Traveling afar to another land,

"Wandering through various states
For over fifty years.
His father, anxious and concerned,
Searched in the four directions.

"Having searched until exhausted,
He settled in a city,
Building a house and dwelling,
Amusing himself with the five desires.

"His household was巨富 [immensely wealthy],
With much gold and silver,
Giant clam shell and agate,
Pearls and lapis lazuli,

"Elephants, horses, oxen, and sheep,
Sedan chairs and carriages,
Farmland estates and servants—
His populace numerous.

"His investments and profits
Extended throughout other lands.
Merchants and traders—
There was no place they were not present.

"Thousands of myriads of billions of multitudes
Surrounded him with reverence.
He was constantly beloved
And cherished by kings.

"Ministers and noble clans
All together honored him.
Due to various causes and conditions,
Those coming and going were numerous.

"Wealthy and powerful as this,
He possessed great influence and power.
Yet as his years declined and he aged,
He increasingly thought anxiously of his son.

"Morning and night he solely contemplated:
'The time of death is approaching.
My foolish son abandoned me
For over fifty years.

"'My storehouses and various possessions—
What shall become of them?'
At that time the impoverished son,
Seeking clothing and food,

"Went from town to town,
From state to state.
Sometimes he obtained something,
Sometimes he obtained nothing.

"Starving, emaciated,
His body afflicted with sores and scabs,
He gradually passed through and traveled,
Arriving at the city where his father dwelt.

"Hiring himself out and wandering,
He finally reached his father's house.
At that time the elder,
Within his gate,

"Spread a great jeweled canopy,
Seated upon a lion throne,
Surrounded by his retinue,
With people attending and guarding him.

"Some were calculating
Gold, silver, and precious objects,
Taking out and bringing in wealth and assets,
Recording and registering documents and tallies.

"The impoverished son, seeing his father
So豪貴 [noble] and honored in his dignity,
Thought he was a king
Or equal to a king.

"Alarmed, afraid, and astonished at himself:
'Why have I come to this place?'
He furthermore thought to himself:
'If I remain here long,

"'I may be oppressed and forced
To be driven and made to work.'
Having contemplated this,
He rushed away and fled,

"Inquiring about an impoverished neighborhood,
Wishing to go there to hire out his labor.
The elder at that time,
Seated upon his lion throne,

"Saw his son from afar
And silently recognized him.
He immediately commanded a messenger
To pursue, seize, and bring him.

"The impoverished son cried out in alarm,
Fainting and collapsing upon the ground:
'This person has seized me—
I shall certainly be killed!

"'Why did the need for clothing and food
Cause me to come to this place?'
The elder, knowing his son
Was foolish, narrow, and inferior,

"Would not believe his words,
Would not believe he was his father.
He immediately employed skillful means
And sent other people—

"Squint-eyed, dwarfed, and ugly,
Without威德 [dignity or virtue]:
'You may speak to him,
Saying we shall hire him,

"'To clear away excrement and filth,
Paying him double wages.'
The impoverished son, hearing this,
Joyfully came along,

"To clear away excrement and filth,
Cleaning all the rooms and chambers.
The elder, through a window,
Constantly saw his son,

"Remembering his son was foolish and inferior,
Delighting in menial tasks.
Then the elder
Put on shabby, soiled clothing,

"Grasping the implement for clearing excrement,
He went to where his son was.
Through skillful means drawing near,
He instructed him to work diligently:

"'I shall furthermore increase your wages
And also provide foot oil.
Food shall be sufficient,
Your mats and bedding thick and warm.'

"With harsh words such as these:
'You should work diligently!'
And also with gentle words:
'You are like my son.'

"The elder, possessing wisdom,
Gradually caused him to enter and exit.
After twenty years,
He managed household affairs,

"Showing him gold and silver,
Pearls and crystal,
And how various objects entered and exited—
All caused him to know this.

"Yet he still dwelt outside the gate,
Lodging in a grass hut,
Thinking of his poverty:
'I do not possess these things.'

"The father, knowing his son's mind
Had gradually become vast and expansive,
Wishing to give him his wealth,
Assembled relatives and clan,

"The king, great ministers,
Kṣatriyas, and householders.
Before this great assembly
He said: 'This is my son,

"'Who abandoned me and went elsewhere
For fifty years.
Since seeing my son return,
Twenty years have already passed.

"'Long ago, in such-and-such a city,
I lost this son.
Wandering about searching for him,
I finally came to this place.

"'All that I possess—
Dwelling and populace—
I completely entrust to him,
For him to use as he wishes.'

"The son, recalling his former poverty,
His inferior and lowly aspiration,
Now, at his father's place,
Has greatly obtained precious jewels,

"Together with dwelling and house,
And all wealth and possessions.
He experiences extreme great joy,
Having attained what he never before had.

"The Buddha too is likewise thus,
Knowing I delight in the lesser.
He never once said:
'You shall all become Buddhas.'

"Instead he said we
Attained the outflow-free [state],
Accomplishing the Lesser Vehicle
As śrāvaka disciples.

"The Buddha commanded us
To expound the supreme Way.
Those who cultivate this
Shall attain Buddhahood.

"Receiving the Buddha's teaching,
For the sake of great Bodhisattvas,
Through various causes and conditions,
And various kinds of parables,

"With manifold expressions,
I expounded the supreme Way.
All the Buddha-sons,
Hearing the Dharma from me,

"Day and night contemplated,
Diligently cultivating and practicing.
At that time all the Buddhas
Immediately conferred predictions upon them:

"'In a future age
You shall attain Buddhahood.'
The secret treasury Dharma
Of all the Buddhas—

"Solely for the sake of Bodhisattvas
Do they演其實事 [demonstrate its true reality],
But not for us
Do they expound this true essential.

"Like that impoverished son
Who drew near to his father,
Though knowing all the possessions,
His mind did not hope to acquire them.

"Though we expound
The Buddha-Dharma treasure,
We ourselves have no aspiration or wish—
We too are likewise thus.

"We considered our inner extinction
Sufficient in itself,
Understanding only this matter—
No other matter beyond it.

"If we heard of purifying Buddha-lands
And teaching and transforming living beings,
We had no delight or joy whatsoever.
Wherefore was this so?

"All the dharmas
Are all empty and tranquil,
Without arising, without ceasing,
Without great, without small,

"Without outflows, without conditioned action.
Contemplating thus,
We did not generate delight or joy.

"We throughout the long night
Regarding Buddha-wisdom
Had no greed, no attachment,
No further aspiration or wish.

"While ourselves regarding the Dharma,
We thought it was ultimate.
We throughout the long night
Cultivated and practiced the Dharma of emptiness,

"Attaining liberation from the triple world's
Suffering and distress,
Dwelling in the final body
With nirvāṇa still remaining.

"The Buddha's teaching and transformation
Obtained the Way without falsity.
We considered this as already having attained
Repayment of the Buddha's kindness.

"Though we, for the sake of
All the Buddha-sons,
Expounded the Bodhisattva Dharma
To seek the Buddha-Way,

"Regarding this Dharma
We永無願樂 [forever had no aspiration or delight].
The guiding teacher allowed us to go,
Observing our minds,

"And initially did not encourage us to advance,
Expounding that there were true benefits.
Like the wealthy elder
Who knew his son's aspirations were inferior,

"Through the power of skillful means
He gently subdued his mind,
And only afterward entrusted to him
All his wealth and possessions.

"The Buddha too is likewise thus,
Manifesting rare occurrences.
Knowing those who delight in the lesser,
Through the power of skillful means

"He tames and subdues their minds,
Then teaches the great wisdom.
We today
Have attained what we never before had—

"Not what we previously hoped for,
Yet now we have obtained it ourselves,
Like that impoverished son
Who obtained immeasurable treasure.

"World-Honored One, I now
Have obtained the Way and obtained the fruit.
Regarding the outflow-free Dharma,
I have obtained pure eyes.

"We throughout the long night
Have upheld the Buddha's pure precepts.
Only today
Have we obtained their fruition and reward.

"Within the Dharma King's Dharma,
Having long cultivated the holy life,
Now we have attained the outflow-free,
Supreme great fruit.

"We now
Truly are śrāvakas,
With the sound of the Buddha-Way
Causing all to hear.

"We now
Truly are Arhats.
Among all the world—
Devas, humans, Māras, and Brahmas—

"Universally among them all
We should receive offerings.
The World-Honored One's great kindness,
Through rare occurrences,

"Showing pity, teaching, and transforming,
Benefiting us—
Throughout immeasurable billions of kalpas,
Who could repay this?

"Offering with hands and feet,
Bowing heads in reverence,
All manner of offerings—
None could repay it.

"Even if bearing [him] on the crown of the head,
Carrying [him] on both shoulders,
Throughout kalpas numerous as Ganges' sands,
Exhausting the mind in reverence,

"Moreover, with fine delicacies,
Immeasurable jeweled garments,
And all manner of bedding,
Various medicinal remedies,

"Ox-head sandalwood
And various precious jewels,
With which to erect stūpas and shrines,
Spreading jeweled garments upon the ground—

"Such matters as these,
Employed to make offerings
Throughout kalpas numerous as Ganges' sands,
Still could not repay it.

"The Buddhas are rare,
Immeasurable, limitless,
Inconceivable,
With great spiritual powers,

"Outflow-free, unconditioned—
Kings of all dharmas.
Able, for the sake of the inferior,
To endure such matters,

"Taking common people attached to marks
And expounding in accordance with what is suitable.
The Buddhas, regarding all dharmas,
Attain utmost sovereignty.

"Knowing all living beings'
Various delights and joys,
Together with their powers of aspiration,
According to what they can bear,

"Through immeasurable parables
They expound the Dharma for them,
According to all living beings'
Past wholesome roots,

"Moreover knowing those mature
And those not yet mature.
Through various kinds of deliberation,
Having made distinctions and knowing this,

"Regarding the path of One Vehicle,
In accordance with what is suitable, they expound three."


CHAPTER FIVE: THE PARABLE OF MEDICINAL HERBS

At that time the World-Honored One addressed Mahākāśyapa and all the great disciples, saying: "Excellent, excellent! Kāśyapa has well spoken of the true and actual merits of the Tathāgata. It is truly as you have said. The Tathāgata indeed possesses immeasurable, limitless, asaṃkhyeya merits. Even if you were to speak of them throughout immeasurable koṭis of kalpas, you could never exhaust them. Kāśyapa, you should know that the Tathāgata is the king of all dharmas. Whatever he expounds is never false. Regarding all dharmas, he expounds them through wisdom and skillful means. Whatever dharmas he expounds all arrive at the ground of omniscient wisdom. The Tathāgata observes and knows all dharmas and where they return and tend. He also knows the deep mental activities of all living beings, penetrating them without obstruction. Moreover, regarding all dharmas he has investigated them thoroughly and comprehended them clearly, manifesting to all living beings the wisdom of omniscience."

"Kāśyapa, it is like the mountains, streams, valleys, and lands of the great trichiliocosm, where the various plants, bushes, forests, and medicinal herbs that are produced are of many kinds, each with different names and colors. Dense clouds spread and extend, everywhere covering the great trichiliocosm, raining down equally and universally. Their moisture permeates everywhere. The plants, bushes, forests, and medicinal herbs—those with small roots, small stems, small branches, and small leaves; those with medium roots, medium stems, medium branches, and medium leaves; those with large roots, large stems, large branches, and large leaves; and all trees, large and small—according to their superior, medium, and inferior nature, each receives its portion. The rain from one cloud, according to the nature of each seed, causes growth, and flowers and fruits open and ripen. Though all are produced from one earth and moistened by one rain, yet all the various plants and trees differ from one another."

"Kāśyapa, you should know the Tathāgata is also like this. He appears in the world like the arising of a great cloud. With his great voice he universally pervades the world of devas, humans, and asuras, just as that great cloud everywhere covers the great trichiliocosm. In the midst of the great assembly he proclaims these words: 'I am the Tathāgata, the Arhat, the Samyak-saṃbuddha, the One Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, the Well-Gone One, the Knower of the World, the Unsurpassed One, the Tamer of Persons, the Teacher of Devas and Humans, the Buddha, the World-Honored One. Those not yet crossed over I cause to cross over. Those not yet liberated I cause to be liberated. Those not yet at peace I cause to be at peace. Those not yet entered into nirvāṇa I cause to attain nirvāṇa. I know the present world and the future world as they truly are. I am the all-knowing one, the all-seeing one, the knower of the Way, the opener of the Way, the expounder of the Way. You devas, humans, and asura assemblies should all come here to hear the Dharma.'"

"At that time, immeasurable thousands of myriads of koṭis of species of living beings came to the Buddha's presence to hear the Dharma. The Tathāgata at that time observed whether the faculties of these beings were sharp or dull, whether they were vigorous or indolent. According to what they were capable of bearing, he expounded the Dharma for them—of innumerable varieties—causing all to rejoice and quickly gain wholesome benefit."

"After these living beings heard this Dharma, they obtained peace and tranquility in the present world and were reborn in wholesome destinies, receiving happiness through the Way. They also were able to hear the Dharma. Having heard the Dharma, they departed from all obstructions. Regarding all dharmas, according to the power within their capacity, they gradually attained entry to the Way—just as that great cloud rains upon all the plants, bushes, forests, and medicinal herbs, and each according to its seed-nature receives the moisture fully and each obtains growth."

"The Dharma expounded by the Tathāgata is of one characteristic and one flavor—namely, the characteristic of liberation, the characteristic of separation, the characteristic of extinction—ultimately arriving at the wisdom of all modes. When living beings hear the Dharma of the Tathāgata, whether upholding it, reading it, reciting it, or cultivating practice according to what is expounded, the merits they obtain they themselves are not aware of. Why is this so? Only the Tathāgata knows the species-characteristics, substance, and nature of these living beings—what matters they are mindful of, what matters they think of, what matters they cultivate; how they are mindful, how they think, how they cultivate; by what dharmas they are mindful, by what dharmas they think, by what dharmas they cultivate, by what dharmas they obtain what dharmas. Living beings abide in various grounds. Only the Tathāgata sees them as they truly are, clearly and without obstruction—just as those plants, bushes, forests, and medicinal herbs do not themselves know their superior, medium, or inferior nature. The Tathāgata knows this Dharma of one characteristic and one flavor—namely, the characteristic of liberation, the characteristic of separation, the characteristic of extinction, the characteristic of ultimate nirvāṇa, the characteristic of constant quiescence and extinction—finally returning to emptiness. Having known this, the Buddha observes the mental desires of living beings and protects them. For this reason he does not immediately expound the wisdom of all modes to them. You, Kāśyapa and others, are exceedingly rare in being able to know that the Tathāgata expounds the Dharma according to what is suitable, and in being able to believe and accept it. Why is this so? The Buddhas, the World-Honored Ones, expound the Dharma according to what is suitable—this is difficult to understand and difficult to know."

At that time the World-Honored One, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse:

"The Dharma King who breaks [the bonds of] existence
Appears in the world,
According to the desires of living beings,
Expounding various dharmas.

"The Tathāgata, venerable and honored,
Whose wisdom is deep and far-reaching,
Has long remained silent on these essential matters,
Not hasty to expound them.

"Those with wisdom, if they hear,
Are able to believe and understand.
Those without wisdom doubt and regret,
And thus are permanently lost.

"Therefore, Kāśyapa,
I expound according to their capacity,
Through various causes and conditions,
Causing them to attain right view.

"Kāśyapa, you should know,
It is like a great cloud
Arising in the world,
Covering everything.

"The cloud of wisdom, containing moisture,
With lightning flashing brilliantly,
Thunder resounding afar,
Causing the multitudes to rejoice.

"The sun's light is obscured,
The earth becomes cool,
Thick clouds descend and spread
As though they could be grasped.

"The rain falls equally,
Coming down on all four sides,
Streaming and pouring without measure,
Thoroughly saturating all the land.

"In mountain streams and险谷,
In secluded and hidden places where they grow,
Plants and medicinal herbs,
Large and small trees,

"Hundreds of grains and seedlings,
Sugarcane and grape vines—
All moistened by the rain,
None not made abundant.

"The parched earth is universally saturated,
Medicinal plants and trees all flourish.
The water produced by that cloud
Is of one flavor.

"Plants, trees, bushes, and forests,
Each receives moisture according to its portion.
All the various trees,
Superior, medium, and inferior,

"According to their greatness or smallness,
Each obtains growth.
Roots, stems, branches, leaves,
Flowers, fruits, luster, and color—

"All reached by the one rain,
All become fresh and glossy.
According to their bodily characteristics,
Their nature, portion, and size,

"Though moistened uniformly,
Each grows luxuriantly.
The Buddha is also like this—
He appears in the world

"Like a great cloud
That universally covers all.
Having appeared in the world,
For the sake of all living beings

"He discriminates and expounds
The reality of all dharmas.
The Great Sage, the World-Honored One,
Among all devas, humans,

"And all other assemblies,
Proclaims these words:
'I am the Tathāgata,
The Most Honored of Two-Legged Beings,

"'Appearing in the world
Like a great cloud,
Fully moistening
All withered living beings,

"'Causing all to depart from suffering,
Obtaining peace, tranquility, and happiness—
The happiness of the world
And the happiness of nirvāṇa.

"'Devas and human assemblies,
With single mind, listen well:
All should come here
To behold the Unsurpassed Honored One.

"'I am the World-Honored One,
Unequaled by any.
To bring peace to living beings,
I appear in the world,

"'For the sake of the great assembly I expound
The sweet-dew pure Dharma.
This Dharma is of one flavor—
Liberation and nirvāṇa.

"'With one wondrous sound
I elucidate this meaning,
Constantly creating causes and conditions
For the Great Vehicle.

"'I regard all [beings]
As entirely equal,
Having no minds
Of preference or aversion.

"'I have no greed or attachment,
Nor any limitations or obstructions.
I constantly, for all,
Expound the Dharma equally—

"'As for one person,
So also for many.
I constantly expound the Dharma,
Never engaged in other matters.

"'Coming and going, sitting and standing,
I am never weary or tired,
Fully satisfying the world
Like rain that universally moistens.

"'Noble and base, superior and inferior,
Those who uphold precepts and those who violate them,
Those complete in deportment
And those not complete,

"'Those of right view and wrong view,
Those of sharp faculties and dull faculties—
I rain the Dharma-rain equally,
Without weariness or fatigue.

"'All living beings
Who hear my Dharma,
According to their capacity receive it,
Dwelling in various grounds.

"'Some dwell among humans and devas,
As wheel-turning sage kings,
As Indra and Brahmā kings—
These are the small medicinal herbs.

"'Those who know the outflow-free dharmas,
Able to attain nirvāṇa,
Who arouse the six spiritual powers
And attain the three clear knowledges,

"'Dwelling alone in mountain forests,
Constantly practicing meditation,
Attaining the realization of Pratyekabuddhas—
These are the medium medicinal herbs.

"'Those who seek the place of the World-Honored One,
[Thinking] "I shall become a Buddha,"
Practicing vigor and concentration—
These are the superior medicinal herbs.

"'Moreover, sons of the Buddha
Who single-mindedly pursue the Buddha-Way,
Constantly practicing compassion,
Knowing for themselves they will become Buddhas,

"'Determined without doubt—
These are called small trees.
Abiding secure in spiritual powers,
Turning the irreversible wheel,

"'Liberating immeasurable koṭis,
Hundreds of thousands of living beings—
Such Bodhisattvas
Are called great trees.

"'The Buddha's equal exposition
Is like the rain of one flavor.
According to the natures of living beings,
What they receive differs—

"'Just as those plants and trees
Each receive differently according to their endowment.
The Buddha uses this parable
To open and reveal through skillful means,

"'Through various kinds of words
Expounding the one Dharma.
Regarding Buddha-wisdom,
[This is] like one drop of the ocean.

"'I rain the Dharma-rain,
Filling the entire world.
The Dharma of one flavor—
Each practices according to capacity,

"'Like those bushes, forests,
And medicinal herbs and trees,
According to their greatness or smallness,
Gradually increasing in luxuriant beauty.

"'The Dharma of all Buddhas
Is constantly of one flavor,
Causing all the worlds
To become fully endowed universally.

"'Gradually cultivating and practicing,
All attain the fruits of the Way.
Śrāvakas and Pratyekabuddhas,
Dwelling in mountain forests,

"'Abiding in the final body,
Hearing the Dharma and attaining its fruit—
These are called medicinal herbs,
Each obtaining increase.

"'If various Bodhisattvas,
Whose wisdom is firm and solid,
Fully understand the three realms
And seek the supreme vehicle—

"'These are called small trees
Obtaining increase.
Again, there are those who abide in meditation,
Attaining spiritual powers,

"'Hearing all dharmas are empty,
Their minds greatly rejoicing,
Emitting countless rays of light,
Liberating all living beings—

"'These are called great trees
Obtaining increase.
Thus, Kāśyapa,
The Dharma expounded by the Buddha

"'Is likened to a great cloud
With the rain of one flavor
Moistening human flowers—
Each attaining ripened fruit.

"Kāśyapa, you should know,
Through various causes and conditions,
Various parables,
I open and reveal the Buddha-Way.

"This is my skillful means,
And all Buddhas are likewise.
Now for your sake
I expound the most real matter:

"The assembly of śrāvakas
Have not all attained extinction.
What you are practicing
Is the Bodhisattva-Way.

"Gradually cultivating and learning,
You shall all accomplish Buddhahood."


═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════


CHAPTER SIX: BESTOWAL OF PROPHECY

At that time, after the World-Honored One spoke these verses, he addressed the great assembly, proclaiming these words: "This disciple of mine, Mahākāśyapa, in a future age shall be able to make offerings to and behold three hundred myriads of koṭis of Buddhas, World-Honored Ones, serving them with offerings, reverence, honor, and praise. He will extensively proclaim the immeasurable great Dharma of all Buddhas. In his final body, he shall accomplish Buddhahood, with the name Raśmiprabhāsa Tathāgata, Arhat, Samyak-saṃbuddha, One Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone One, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed One, Tamer of Persons, Teacher of Devas and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One. His land will be called Raśmiguṇa, and the kalpa will be called Mahāvyūha. The lifespan of that Buddha will be twelve intermediate kalpas. The True Dharma will abide in the world for twenty intermediate kalpas, and the Semblance Dharma will likewise abide for twenty intermediate kalpas. His land will be magnificently adorned, without any defilements or evil things, without tiles, stones, thorns, or brambles, or excrement and impurity. The land will be level and even, without any high or low places, pits, valleys, or mounds. The ground will be of lapis lazuli. Jeweled trees will be arrayed in rows. Golden ropes will border the paths. Scattered precious flowers will be everywhere, all pure and clean. The Bodhisattvas of that land will number immeasurable thousands of koṭis. The assembly of śrāvakas will likewise be immeasurable. There will be no activities of Māra, though Māra and Māra's subjects will be present—all will protect the Buddha-Dharma."

At that time the World-Honored One, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse:

"I announce to the bhikṣus:
With the Buddha-eye
I see this Kāśyapa—
In a future age,

"After immeasurable kalpas,
He shall accomplish Buddhahood.
In ages to come
He will make offerings to and behold

"Three hundred myriads of koṭis
Of Buddhas, World-Honored Ones.
For the sake of Buddha-wisdom,
Purely cultivating the holy practices,

"Having made offerings to the most supreme
Two-Legged Honored Ones,
He will cultivate and learn
All the unsurpassed wisdom.

"In his final body
He shall accomplish Buddhahood.
His land will be pure,
With lapis lazuli for ground.

"Many jeweled trees
Will be arrayed along the paths,
Golden ropes bordering the ways,
Pleasing to all who see them.
